    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer14993996606215181670">New Directions celebrates the Pablo Neruda Centennial. In celebration of the 100th anniversary of Pablo Neruda's birth, New Directions is pleased to announce the reissue of a classic work in a timeless translation by Donald D. Walsh and fully bilingual.<br/><br/><i>The Captain's Verses</i> was first published anonymously in 1952, some years before Neruda married Matilde Urrutia - the one w</span>
  <span id="freeText14993996606215181670" style="display:none">New Directions celebrates the Pablo Neruda Centennial. In celebration of the 100th anniversary of Pablo Neruda's birth, New Directions is pleased to announce the reissue of a classic work in a timeless translation by Donald D. Walsh and fully bilingual.<br /><br /><i>The Captain's Verses</i> was first published anonymously in 1952, some years before Neruda married Matilde Urrutia - the one with "the fire / of an unchained meteor" - to whom he had addressed these poems of love, ecstasy, devotion, and fury. Our bilingual edition is seen by many as the most intimate and passionate volume of Neruda's love poetry, capturing all the erotic energy of a new love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14993996606215181670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer13984633353746757647">Los versos del capitán = The Captain's Verses, Pablo Neruda<br/>Pablo Neruda, winner of the Nobel Prize for Literature, finished writing The Captain's Verses in 1952 while in exile on the island of Capri--the paradisal setting for the blockbuster film Il Postino (The Postman). Surrounded by sea, sun, and Capri's natural splendors, Neruda addressed these poems to his lover Matilde Urrutia before they were married, but didn't publish them publicly until 1963. This complete, bilingual collection has bec</span>
  <span id="freeText13984633353746757647" style="display:none">Los versos del capitán = The Captain's Verses, Pablo Neruda<br />Pablo Neruda, winner of the Nobel Prize for Literature, finished writing The Captain's Verses in 1952 while in exile on the island of Capri--the paradisal setting for the blockbuster film Il Postino (The Postman). Surrounded by sea, sun, and Capri's natural splendors, Neruda addressed these poems to his lover Matilde Urrutia before they were married, but didn't publish them publicly until 1963. This complete, bilingual collection has become a classic for love-struck readers around the world--passionately sensuous, and exploding with all the erotic energy of a new love.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین نگرش: ماه اکتبر سال 1996 میلادی<br />عنوان: هوا را از من بگیر خنده‌ ات را نه!‏ - گزینه شعرهای عاشقانه؛ شاعر: پابلو نرودا؛ مترجم: احمد پوری؛ تهران، نشر چشمه، 1374؛ در 104 ص؛ چاپ سوم 1377؛ شابک: ایکس - 964619432؛ عنوان دیگر: آوازهای ناخدا؛ چاپ چهارم 1378؛ چاپ چنجم و ششم 1380؛ هفتم 1381؛ هشتم 1382؛ دوازدهم و سیزدهم 1385؛ هفدهم و هیجدهم 1387؛ نوزدهم 1388؛ بیستم 1387؛ بیست و یکم 1389؛ بیست و دوم 1390؛ شابک: 9789646194328؛ چاپ بیست و پنجم 1393؛ موضوع: شعر شاعران اسپانیایی - سده 20 م<br /><br /><b>هوا را از من بگیر خنده‌ ات را نه</b><br />هوا را از من بگیر خنده‌ ات را نه!؛<br />نان را از من بگیر، اگر می‌خواهی<br />هوا را از من بگیر، اما<br />خنده‌ات را نه<br /> گل سرخ را از من بگیر<br />سوسنی را که می‌کاری<br />آبی را که به ناگاه<br />در شادی تو سرریز می‌کند<br /><br />موجی ناگهانی از نقره را<br />که در تو می‌زاید<br />از پس نبردی سخت باز می‌گردم<br />با چشمانی خسته<br />که دنیا را دیده است<br />بی هیچ دگرگونی<br />اما خنده‌ ات که رها می‌شود<br />و پروازکنان در آسمان مرا می‌جوید<br />تمامی درهای زندگی را<br />به رویم می‌گشاید<br />عشق من، خنده ی تو<br />در تاریکترین لحظه‌ ها می‌شکند<br /><br />و اگر دیدی، به ناگاه<br />خون من بر سنگفرش خیابان جاری است<br />بخند، زیرا خنده ی تو<br />برای دستان من <br />شمشیری ست آخته<br /><br />خنده ی تو، در پاییز<br />در کناره دریا<br />موج کف‌ آلوده‌ اش را<br />باید برافروزد؛<br /><br />و در بهاران، عشق من<br />خنده ات را می‌خواهم<br />چون گلی که در انتظارش بودم <br /><br />بخند بر شب<br />بر روز، بر ماه<br />بخند بر پیچاپیچ خیابان‌های جزیره؛<br />بر این پسر بچه ی کمرو<br />که دوستت دارد<br /><br />اما آنگاه که چشم می‌گشایم و می‌بندم؛<br />آنگاه که پاهایم می‌روند و باز می‌گردند<br /><br />نان را، هوا را<br />روشنی را، بهار را<br />از من بگیر<br /><br />اما خنده‌ ات را هرگز!؛<br /><br />تا چشم از دنیا نبندم<br />پابلو نرودا<br />ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1147251054" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1147251054" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1147251054?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218214" href="/book/show/6261228-the-captain-s-verses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=verse">verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=hispanic">hispanic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1147251054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3029179911926890572"><i>Love, a question<br/>has destroyed you.</i><br/><br/>Love, Desire, Furies, – it is in these elemental emotions Pablo Neruda has cast in verse his and the life of his beloved. They met at the beginning of Time when Big Bang was taking shape in their souls and when those various elements came together, guided by violent forces, in a restive arrangement to from their bodies out which arose a desire of self-preservation in perpetual conflict with the vein of self-abnegation. In a way these poems represent the struggle</span>
  <span id="freeText3029179911926890572" style="display:none"><i>Love, a question<br />has destroyed you.</i><br /><br />Love, Desire, Furies, – it is in these elemental emotions Pablo Neruda has cast in verse his and the life of his beloved. They met at the beginning of Time when Big Bang was taking shape in their souls and when those various elements came together, guided by violent forces, in a restive arrangement to from their bodies out which arose a desire of self-preservation in perpetual conflict with the vein of self-abnegation. In a way these poems represent the struggle of emotions - emotions that are not conflicting but complementing, but when they overstep their sphere of influence, they disturb that of others. A veritable struggle then ensues in the cosmos of heart. Imagine, what it would feel like if love took on the destructive force of fury? Or if love turned out to be no more than the <i>nom de guerre</i> of a transient desire? Neruda will let you find the answer for yourself. <br /><br />Neruda had written these poems for his wife Matilde Urrutia at a time when sublime love was feeling the first pains of domestic disquiet. He published the collection anonymously and did not take ownership for over a decade, perhaps because he considered them confessional poems. He wrote: “To reveal its source was to strip bare the intimacy of its birth.” But his close friends, seeing the success of the poems, persuaded him to let the personal become the public – and universal. This is what good art is: it is personal at heart but in its scope it is universal, so that it becomes intimately personal to whoever lays eyes on it. <br /><br />I have read this collection twice and on both occasions I could not detect anything that makes these poems confessional. Allusions to people and events are completely missing, if that was the fear. There is also no trace of Neruda the man distinguishable from Neruda the poet whom we do not already know from his other collections, in style and form, and in the use of language and metaphor. One may see this collection as an afterword of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1147252278?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair</a>. My suspicion is that since, unlike the rest of his poetry, these express his difficulties with love and loving, leading on to a visible conflict that may be seen as an unsuitable topic for love poetry, Neruda did not like what he was writing and hid himself. <br /><br />Be that as it may, amid the pleasures and bounties of boundless love expressed in a colossal voice of nature, of which I will write further down, here Neruda writes freely of the <i>nooks of shadow</i> darkening the path which he wants <i>as straight as road or a sword</i>; his imagination struggles with <i>each wound that has the shape of your mouth</i>, which has injured him in his slumber of love; he complains openly, <i>You have not made me suffer / merely wait</i> and <i>I vainly sought you in the depths of my arms;</i> but he’s also repentant for his own part, <i>Shake off my word that came to wound you / and let it fly through the open window / It will return to wound me / without your guiding it.</i> In the end he affirms, <i>It is not only the fire that burns between us / but all of life;</i> Then comes the final warning, when love is pushed against the wall, it resorts to masculine violence:<br /><br />"I shall end up by attacking<br />those who between my breast and your fragrance<br />try to interpose their dark foot."<br /><br />Because<br /><br />"Ah let them tell me how<br />I could abolish you<br />and let my hands without your form<br />tear the fire from my words."<br /><br />This is then love at a loss, passion circumventing the trappings of stillness, vow fighting the pangs of doubt, affection scratching off the rust of weariness; this is, to use Milan Kundera’s phrase, Neruda’s attempt to understand the unbearable lightness of love. He does this in a manner which is his forte: by transforming his beloved into Nature pure and unsullied, made up of earth, water, fire and wind, mountains, rivers, seas and skies, and all that emerges from those. In short, he traverses every corner of the body of earth to sow it and cultivate it, to sow and cultivate again and again, to save it from the barrenness that threatens its primordial fecundity. Neruda, as he does elsewhere, employs a stunning and all-encompassing telluric metaphor to the soul and body of his beloved. Take a look at this:<br /><br /><b>In You the Earth</b><br /><br />Little<br />rose,<br />roselet,<br />at times,<br />tiny and naked,<br />it seems<br />as though you would fit<br />in one of my hands,<br />as though I’ll clasp you like this<br />and carry you to my mouth,<br />but<br />suddenly<br />my feet touch your feet and my mouth your lips:<br />you have grown,<br />your shoulders rise like two hills,<br />your breasts wander over my breast,<br />my arm scarcely manages to encircle the thin<br />new-moon line of your waist:<br />in love you have loosened yourself like sea water:<br />I can scarcely measure the sky’s most spacious eyes<br />and I lean down to your mouth to kiss the earth.<br /><br />A beautiful, delightful poem loaded with creative eros whose most pleasing aspect is the shift of perspective that gradually expands from a 'rose, roselet’ to ‘loosened sea water’ and ‘spacious sky.’<br /><br />One may object to his unabashed masculinity, the latent violence of his possessive charms, the overpowering candour of his physical superiority, the machismo he infuses his confessions of love with – his manliness tightens, without a qualm, its insistent arms against the tender flesh of the lover’s body <i>lying in surrender.</i> His poems are written for the elemental female form, testing her patience with the flood and volcano of emotions that he pours into it. But he is not unaware of this. He understands it thus:<br /><br />From <b>Absence</b><br /><br />"I have scarcely left you<br />when you go in me, crystalline<br />or trembling,<br />or uneasy, wounded by me<br />or overwhelmed with love, as when your eyes<br />close upon the gift of life<br />that without cease I give you.<br /><br />Yet, he pays homage to the female form of his lover with a lightness of expression that dilutes his aggressive beginnings. Take a look at this one by way of example. Here, again, the perspective transform the lover from the 'little one' to 'the earth at vintage time', vast and pristine, naked and without limits. <br /><br /><b>The Infinite One</b><br /><br />“Do you see these hands? They have measured<br />the earth, they have separated<br />minerals and cereals,<br />they have made peace and war,<br />they have demolished the distances<br />of all the seas and rivers,<br />and yet,<br />when they move over you,<br />little one,<br />grain of wheat, swallow,<br />they cannot encompass you,<br />they are weary seeking<br />the twin doves<br />that rest or fly in your breast,<br />they travel the distance of your legs,<br />they coil in the light of your waist.<br />For me you are a treasure more laden<br />with immensity than the sea and its branches<br />and you are white and blue and spacious like<br />the earth at vintage time.<br />In that territory,<br />from your feet to your brow,<br />walking, walking, walking,<br />I shall spend my life."<br /><br />Pablo writes, Jibran rates, but how come a star goes missing? As noted, Neruda had written these poems as anonymous specimens soon to be forgotten for good. In that some poems are doubtless written in haste. In some lines poetry is difficult to detect; in others there is repetitive enumeration of emotions and elements that smacks of poetic juvenilia. I can easily overlook it for the journey has been full of brilliant scenery, fresh metaphor and uninhibited expression of love and fidelity that ends in these words:<br /><br />"And so this letter ends<br />with no sadness<br />my feet are firm upon the earth,<br />my hand writes this letter on the road,<br />and in the midst of life I shall be<br />always<br />beside the friend, facing the enemy,<br />with your name on my mouth<br />and a kiss that never<br />broke away from yours."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3029179911926890572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15815893585822804949">Undoubtedly some of the finest love poems ever written and probably Neruda’s masterpiece (my opinion).<br/><br/>"I want you to know<br/>one thing.<br/><br/>You know how this is:<br/>if I look<br/>at the crystal moon, at the red branch<br/>of the slow autumn at my window,<br/>if I touch<br/>near the fire<br/>the impalpable ash<br/>or the wrinkled body of the log,<br/>everything carries me to you,<br/>as if everything that exists:<br/>aromas, light, metals,<br/>were little boats that sail<br/>toward those isles of yours that wait for me.<br/><br/>Well, now,<br/>if little by little you stop lovei</span>
  <span id="freeText15815893585822804949" style="display:none">Undoubtedly some of the finest love poems ever written and probably Neruda’s masterpiece (my opinion).<br /><br />"I want you to know<br />one thing.<br /><br />You know how this is:<br />if I look<br />at the crystal moon, at the red branch<br />of the slow autumn at my window,<br />if I touch<br />near the fire<br />the impalpable ash<br />or the wrinkled body of the log,<br />everything carries me to you,<br />as if everything that exists:<br />aromas, light, metals,<br />were little boats that sail<br />toward those isles of yours that wait for me.<br /><br />Well, now,<br />if little by little you stop loveing me<br />I shall stop loving you little by little.<br /><br />If suddenly<br />you forget me<br />do not look for me,<br />for I shall already have forgotten you.<br /><br />If you think it long and mad,<br />the wind of banners<br />that passes through my life,<br />and you decide<br />to leave me at the shore<br />of the heart where I have roots,<br />remember<br />that on that day,<br />at that hour,<br />I shall lift my arms<br />and my roots will set off<br />to seek another land.<br /><br />But<br />if each day,<br />each hour,<br />you feel that you are destined for me<br />with implacable sweetness,<br />if each day a flower<br />climbs up to your lips to seek me,<br />ah my love, ah my own,<br />in me all that fire is repeated,<br />in me nothing is extinguished or forgotten,<br />my love feeds on your love, beloved,<br />and as long as you live it will be in your arms<br />without leaving mine."</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review69938404">

<span id="freeTextContainer13207825164022757166">Neruda was such an incredible poet. The Captain's Verses is one of his most incredible and delectable collections of poems. One wants to cry and dance and scream when reading his limpid, beautiful lyricism. To be read again and again and again throughout life to remind oneself of true beauty and intense desire.</span>

  <span id="freeText18336411915458439895" style="display:none"><br />I've read beautiful things from Pablo Neruda, but I can't say I enjoyed this one and how he connected everything with Nature and things surrounding it. I just didn't enjoy the way he put things together.<br /><br />There is one poem I loved, though:<br /><br /> <b> <i> El Monte Y El Río </i></b> <br /><br /> <i><br /> En mi patria hay un monte.<br /> En mi patria hay un río.<br /><br /> Ven conmigo.<br /><br /> La noche al monte sube.<br /> El hambre baja al río.<br /><br /> Ven conmigo.<br /><br /> Quiénes son los que sufren?<br /> No sé, pero son míos.<br /><br /> Ven conmigo.<br /><br /> No sé, pero me llaman<br /> y me dicen "Sufrimos".<br /><br /> Ven conmigo.<br /><br /> Y me dicen: "Tu pueblo,<br /> tu pueblo desdichado,<br /> entre el monte y el río,<br /><br /> con hambre y con dolores,<br /> no quiere luchar solo,<br /> te está esperando, amigo".<br /><br /> Oh tú, la que yo amo,<br /> pequeña, grano rojo<br /> de trigo,<br /> será dura la lucha,<br /> la vida será dura,<br /> pero vendrás conmigo. </i><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_544941439" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/544941439" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1597887-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498588219p2/1597887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544941439?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/1597887-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1597887-john?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544941439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2163566585822538011">When I was a young man, I picked up this volume of Neruda's poetry. I was around 20 years old (so far as I can remember), and I was trying to widen my worldview. I remember reading Neruda's poems and wanting--desperately wanting--to find something deep and meaningful in them. I dove in relentlessly, fanning through the book and pulling them out in random fashion. My mind devoured them eagerly. My mind found nothing in the poems that it responded to.<br/><br/>Recently I have decided to revisit some books t</span>
  <span id="freeText2163566585822538011" style="display:none">When I was a young man, I picked up this volume of Neruda's poetry. I was around 20 years old (so far as I can remember), and I was trying to widen my worldview. I remember reading Neruda's poems and wanting--desperately wanting--to find something deep and meaningful in them. I dove in relentlessly, fanning through the book and pulling them out in random fashion. My mind devoured them eagerly. My mind found nothing in the poems that it responded to.<br /><br />Recently I have decided to revisit some books that I read when I was a young man. I picked this one up again, feeling a sense of trepidation. Would I find something in the poems this time? Would I feel the same flatness as before?<br /><br />I am happy to report that Neruda deserves his reputation. When I was a young man, I thought and read as a young man; I processed the poems by trying to analyze them, solely digging under the surface and into the language. My mind sought as a young mind; my mind reported nothing there. With more miles having passed under my feet, and with the strain of love and lost desire upon my heart, something in Neruda's work resonates in me. My mind still seeks, and it finds ample purchase in many of the poems, but my heart--a heart that understands the fatal tragedies of life's performances, both the true and false ones--it, this soul in me, finds a vibration in his work that sounds something in my heart as well. <br /><br />Perhaps it is the set of poems on love; perhaps it is the set of poems on 'The Furies'; perhaps it is the diction, the soft repetition of 'amor, amor, amor'; perhaps it is some undefinable thing that holds its meaning 'like water in a net / the surface and the temperature / of the purest wave of life'; perhaps it is the suffering, the longing, the sort of hard, virile anguish that Neruda finds in his work; perhaps it would be these things that start that vibration. Perhaps it is just sad memory in me that hears something similar in him.<br /><br />Whatever it is, I cannot recommend this book enough. Read it and then put it away for years. Find it again some day, as I feel sure that I will once more in years to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2163566585822538011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218214" href="/book/show/6261228-the-captain-s-verses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296602451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3500160171280723518">Have you ever been in love? Or rather, how many times have you been in love?<br/><br/>Neruda's <i>The Captain's Verses</i> was a shipwreck of emotions yet a cruise ship to the islands of desire, devotion, and dreams. What set apart Neruda from other poets was his ability to make something extraordinary out of the simple things; an appreciation of gestures, everyday-entities unnoticed; a love all of us, at one time, have the luck to feel yet struggle to define and show. Comparing this collection to <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2118775748?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Twenty Love</a></i></span>
  <span id="freeText3500160171280723518" style="display:none">Have you ever been in love? Or rather, how many times have you been in love?<br /><br />Neruda's <i>The Captain's Verses</i> was a shipwreck of emotions yet a cruise ship to the islands of desire, devotion, and dreams. What set apart Neruda from other poets was his ability to make something extraordinary out of the simple things; an appreciation of gestures, everyday-entities unnoticed; a love all of us, at one time, have the luck to feel yet struggle to define and show. Comparing this collection to <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2118775748?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despire</a>
</i>, it encompassed and grasped a much sophisticated and thorough romantic reverie. It was both the morning sunlight and the shadow cast by the moonlight. For me, this was Liszt's music as it was for the ears and the soul and this was for the eyes <i>and</i> the soul. <br /><br />Some of Neruda's one-liners stay with you like cherished Sunday walks in the park, holding hands along the streets while trees gently sway with the wind, and late night talks when sleep feels like a dream with the beloved from "I did not suffer, my love, I was only waiting for you." to "Love, a question has destroyed you"<br /><br />Falling in love has never been this glaring and scintillating. This collection, most importantly, made me experience falling in love again (if not to relive then to recapture it) — with all of its creases and presses included. <br /><br />Some excerpts I would love to keep in my pockets and share to the next person:<br /><br />** "in love you have loosened yourself like sea water:<br />I can scarcely measure the sky's most spacious eyes<br />and I lean down to your mouth to kiss the earth"<br />— from IN YOU THE EARTH<br /><br />** "But I love your feet<br />only because they walked <br />upon the earth and upon<br />the wind and upon the waters,<br />until they found me"<br />— from FEET<br /><br />** "I have scarcely left you<br />when you go in me, crystalline,<br />or trembling,<br />or uneasy, wounded by me<br />or overwhelmed with love, as when your eyes<br />close upon the gift of life<br />that without cease I give you."<br />— from ABSENCE<br /><br />** "I want you straight as<br />the sword or the road.<br /><br />But you insist<br />on keeping a nook<br />of shadow that I do not want."<br />— from THE QUESTION<br /><br />** "My love, what do you find<br />in your closed well?<br />Seaweed, swamps, rocks?<br />What do you see with blind eyes,<br />bitter and wounded?<br /><br />Darling, you will not find<br />in the well into which you fall<br />what I keep for you on the heights:<br />a bouquet of dewy jasmines,<br />a kiss deeper than your abyss."<br />— from THE WELL<br /><br />** "But<br />there are other memories,<br />not only flowers from the fire<br />but little sprouts<br />that suddenly appear<br />when I go on trains <br />or in the streets."<br />— from NOT ONLY THE FIRE</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2035227457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8397753887694079474"><i>your beauty then<br/>not only lights the fire<br/>that burns unquenched among us<br/>but with your love it is calling to me<br/>and across your life<br/>it is giving me the life that I lack<br/>and to the taste of your love is added the clay,<br/>the kiss of the earth that waits for me.<br/><br/>~from </i>Little America<br/><br/>This book was quite beautiful. I've been meaning to read some Neruda ever since I visited his LOVELY houses in Chile (he was Chilean, the first South American to be a Nobel Laureate (fun fact)) -- <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://sparrowsflysouth.blogspot.co.uk/2016/03/south-american-adventure.html'>https://sparrowsflysouth.blogsp</a></span>
  <span id="freeText8397753887694079474" style="display:none"><i>your beauty then<br />not only lights the fire<br />that burns unquenched among us<br />but with your love it is calling to me<br />and across your life<br />it is giving me the life that I lack<br />and to the taste of your love is added the clay,<br />the kiss of the earth that waits for me.<br /><br />~from </i>Little America<br /><br />This book was quite beautiful. I've been meaning to read some Neruda ever since I visited his LOVELY houses in Chile (he was Chilean, the first South American to be a Nobel Laureate (fun fact)) -- <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://sparrowsflysouth.blogspot.co.uk/2016/03/south-american-adventure.html">https://sparrowsflysouth.blogspot.co....</a> . I have to say, the book wasn't quite as impressive as the houses, but I did enjoy it! <i>Las Vidas</i> was my favourite section. Some of the imagery was very powerful.<br /><br />I did find him arrogant and self-centred at times -- <i>"You know that I am not just one man / but all men"</i> (The Dead Woman) -- as if his lover Mathilde, the subject of this book, exists only for him, as a backdrop to his struggles. He often described her in terms of the earth, which was sometimes empowering (like in Little America, one of my favourite poems), but sometimes objectifying. Also, he used the same language a lot throughout, to the point where it felt like every poem was just earth-sky-land-breasts-love-hair-nipples-wheat-gold-clay. But I still really liked this book, and would read Neruda again.<br /><br /><i>life,<br />afterward, could put all<br />the seas and the earth<br />between us, and we could come together<br />in spite of space,<br />step by step seeking each other,<br />from once ocean to another, <br />until I saw that the sky was aflame<br />and your hair was flying in the light<br />and you came to my kisses with the fire<br />of an unchained meteor<br />and as you melted in my blood, the sweetness<br />of the wild plum<br />of our childhood I received in my mouth,<br />and I clutched you to my breast as<br />if I were regaining earth and life.<br /><br />~ from</i> Odes and Burgeonings</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489188281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3180731092846290657">Nobel Laureate Pablo Neruda was an activist in Chile, but he also wrote some of the world's great poems of love. I purchased this edition of The Captain's Verses because it gives the poems in both the original Spanish (lovely lyric flow) and in English. He was a master of imagery who bared his soul in words. His poems burn bright, cut deep and can make me weep. Read them aloud and see.<br/>I'll give some samples from various poems. Nothing else can really express what his poems can do.<br/>From "The Dream</span>
  <span id="freeText3180731092846290657" style="display:none">Nobel Laureate Pablo Neruda was an activist in Chile, but he also wrote some of the world's great poems of love. I purchased this edition of The Captain's Verses because it gives the poems in both the original Spanish (lovely lyric flow) and in English. He was a master of imagery who bared his soul in words. His poems burn bright, cut deep and can make me weep. Read them aloud and see.<br />I'll give some samples from various poems. Nothing else can really express what his poems can do.<br />From "The Dream": "Walking on the sands/ I decided to leave you./ I was treading a dark clay/ that trembled/ and I, sinking and coming out, / decided that you should come out/ of me, that you were weighing me down/ like a cutting stone,/ and I worked out your loss/ step by step:/ to cut off your roots,/ to release you alone into the wind."<br />From "If You Forget Me": "I want you to know/ one thing. /You know how this is:/ if I look/ at the crystal moon, at the red branch/ of the slow autumn at my window,/ if I touch/ near the fire/ the impalpable ash/ or the wrinkled body of the log,/ everything carries me to you, / as if everything that exists,/ aromas, light, metals,/ were little boats that sail/ toward those isles of yours that wait for me."<br />The ending of "Letter On the Road": "And so this letter ends/ with no sadness:/ my feet are firm upon the earth,/ my hand writes this letter on the road,/ and in the midst of life I shall be/ always/ beside the friend, facing the enemy,/ with your name on my mouth/ and a kiss that never/ broke aways from yours."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2102151330" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2102151330" />
    <a title="Noah Goats" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41735508-noah-goats"><img alt="Noah Goats" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526489093p2/41735508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2102151330?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Noah Goats" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Noah Goats" href="/user/show/41735508-noah-goats">Noah Goats</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781504798747" href="/book/show/34351937-los-versos-del-capitan">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2102151330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13799878922558792571">Some of these poems read a lot like the sort of schmaltzy nonsense that might be written by a lovelorn high school student, others are a bit better than that. It's hard to see how Neruda ever won a Nobel prize if this is representative of his work. <br/><br/>Quítame el pan, si quieres,<br/>Quítame el aire, pero<br/>No me quitas tu risa.<br/><br/>There are a lot of passages here that sound like second hand pop music lyrics. Of course, Spanish my second language, and when I read in Spanish the words don't have quite the same s</span>
  <span id="freeText13799878922558792571" style="display:none">Some of these poems read a lot like the sort of schmaltzy nonsense that might be written by a lovelorn high school student, others are a bit better than that. It's hard to see how Neruda ever won a Nobel prize if this is representative of his work. <br /><br />Quítame el pan, si quieres,<br />Quítame el aire, pero<br />No me quitas tu risa.<br /><br />There are a lot of passages here that sound like second hand pop music lyrics. Of course, Spanish my second language, and when I read in Spanish the words don't have quite the same soul shaking power English words do for me, so, maybe I just don't get it... but, I don't think so. I think these are mediocre poems.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13799878922558792571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3249161693" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3249161693" />
    <a title="Arushi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31923990-arushi"><img alt="Arushi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455967551p2/31923990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3249161693?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arushi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arushi" href="/user/show/31923990-arushi">Arushi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31923990-arushi?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31923990-arushi?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31923990-arushi?shelf=world">world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3249161693" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8893389058009454210">What most people don't know about me, and I don't acknowledge frequently either is what a sucker I am for romance. There are plenty of days when I want to write long letters, get lightly drunk on wine and dance to Leonard Cohen, and talk late into the night. When I feel that lump in my throat and that longing grows, I listen to a certain kind of music. When it increases more, I try writing or painting. When it becomes so intense that it seems unbearable, I read poetry like this. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2115870853" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2115870853" />
    <a title="Autumn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6437152-autumn"><img alt="Autumn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542930799p2/6437152.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2115870853?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Autumn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Autumn" href="/user/show/6437152-autumn">Autumn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811218214" href="/book/show/6261228-the-captain-s-verses">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2115870853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2450161687355735468">I really wanted to like this collection, but struggled after reading the same metaphors in each poem. Like, I get it Pablo-her mountainous body reminds you of wheat, and you really like her boobs. She pierces you like the thorny flower she is, and you bleed in jealous, possessive agony. But your love is just so strong, it transcends borders, crosses through time and space, and it blooms into the most beautiful garden. Your love that-I GET IT ALREADY, STOP WITH THE METAPHORS.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer16087275333473650050">OMG, what do I say about my favorite male poet? "If little by little you stop loving me, then little by little I shall stop loving you." Those are his words I have lived by in one of my favorite poems Iloved for years. I have learned Spanish thru these beautifully cascading poems. And it is wonderful to find a person with whom you feel you can share these treasured and wise poems.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8cvXXK4Z0UA558kKYSZMnyBZsUo2U63ZNFrr9oyv3r9nWVle9V72gFsZezNxrML0P+s88VVGUVoBtkXnfnGPOw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_109237'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/109237.Selected_Poems?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Selected Poems<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Jorge Luis Borges<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.35 avg rating &mdash; 5,842 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1971\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18311210105948549665\">Though universally acclaimed for his dazzling works of fictions, Jorge Luis Borges always considered himself first and foremost a poet. This new bilingual selection brings together some two hundred poems--the largest collection of Borges\' poetry ever<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18311210105948549665\" style=\"display:none\">Though universally acclaimed for his dazzling works of fictions, Jorge Luis Borges always considered himself first and foremost a poet. This new bilingual selection brings together some two hundred poems--the largest collection of Borges\' poetry ever assembled in English, including scores of poems never previously translated. Edited by Alexander Coleman, the selection draws from a lifetime\'s work--from Borges\' first published volume of verse, Fervor de Buenos Aires (1923), to his final work, Los Conjurados, published just a year before his death in 1986. Throughout this unique collection the brilliance of the Spanish originals is matched by luminous English versions by a remarkable cast of translators, including Robert Fitzgerald, Stephen Kessler, W. S. Merwin, Alastair Reid, Mark Strand, Charles Tomlinson, and John Updike.For more than seventy years, Penguin has been the leading publisher of classic literature in the English-speaking world. Alternating between tough and gentle, sensitive and gritty, Bukowski lays bare the myriad facets of love—its selfishness and its narcissism, its randomness, its mystery and its misery, and, ultimately, its true joyfulness, endurance and redemptive power.\n\nBukowski is brilliant on love—often amusing, sometimes playful, and fleetingly sweet. On Love offers deep insight into Bukowski the man and the artist; whether writing about his daughter, his lover, his friends, or his work, he is piercingly honest and poignantly reflective, using love as a prism to see the world in all its beauty and cruelty, and his own fragile place in it. “My love is a hummingbird sitting that quiet moment on the bough,” he writes, “as the same cat       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">About Pablo Neruda</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1499982432p3/4026.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">Pablo Neruda
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
7,729
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2727454153683499498">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be fro</span>
  <span id="freeText2727454153683499498" style="display:none">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be from Paul Verlaine. With his works translated into many languages, Pablo Neruda is considered one of the greatest and most influential poets of the 20th century.<br /><br />Neruda was accomplished in a variety of styles, ranging from erotically charged love poems like his collection Twenty Poems of Love and a Song of Despair, surrealist poems, historical epics, and overtly political manifestos. In 1971 Neruda won the Nobel Prize for Literature, a controversial award because of his political activism. Colombian novelist Gabriel García Márquez once called him "the greatest poet of the 20th century in any language."<br /><br />On July 15, 1945, at Pacaembu Stadium in São Paulo, Brazil, he read to 100,000 people in honor of Communist revolutionary leader Luís Carlos Prestes. When Neruda returned to Chile after his Nobel Prize acceptance speech, Salvador Allende invited him to read at the Estadio Nacional before 70,000 people.<br /><br />During his lifetime, Neruda occupied many diplomatic posts and served a stint as a senator for the Chilean Communist Party. When Conservative Chilean President González Videla outlawed communism in Chile, a warrant was issued for Neruda's arrest. Friends hid him for months in a house basement in the Chilean port of Valparaíso. Later, Neruda escaped into exile through a mountain pass near Maihue Lake into Argentina. Years later, Neruda was a close collaborator to socialist President Salvador Allende.<br /><br />Neruda was hospitalized with cancer at the time of the Chilean coup d'état led by Augusto Pinochet. Three days after being hospitalized, Neruda died of heart failure. Already a legend in life, Neruda's death reverberated around the world. Pinochet had denied permission to transform Neruda's funeral into a public event. However, thousands of grieving Chileans disobeyed the curfew and crowded the streets to pay their respects. Neruda's funeral became the first public protest against the Chilean military dictatorship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2727454153683499498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
